The Hanafi Definition of the Quran: Why Two Conditions Suffice
Exploring alternative definitions of the Quran

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of the Quran and explain why the conditions of being written in the mushafs and transmitted by tawatur are necessary and sufficient.

Core Idea
The Quran is the revealed text written in the mushafs and transmitted by tawatur; inimitability is not part of its operative definition.

How do we draw a clear line between the Quran and other texts? Usul scholars have proposed multiple attributes. This passage shows why the Hanafi tradition settles on two precise conditions – inscription in the standardized mushafs and mass transmission (tawatur) – to avoid ambiguity and exclude irregular readings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core issue
What is the Quran? Scholars need a definition that distinguishes it from hadith, other revealed books, and variant readings. The definition must be both precise and practical.
2
List the proposed definitions
Some scholars defined it with inimitability and being revealed. Others added three attributes: revealed, written in mushafs, transmitted by tawatur. The author uses only two: written in mushafs and mutawatir.
Each definition reflects a different view of what is essential.
3
Analyze the necessity of each condition
The condition 'written in the mushafs' excludes hadith and other books. 'Mutawatir' excludes irregular (shadhdh) readings. Inimitability is not needed because it is not present in every part and is not understood by common people.
The author argues that two conditions suffice for the purpose of distinction.
4
Understand the thamarah (practical consequence)
This definition means that only the 'Uthmanic mushaf text transmitted by tawatur is Quran. Recitations like those of Ubayy ibn Ka'b or Ibn Mas'ud that have extra words are not Quran and cannot be recited in prayer.
The definition directly impacts fiqh rulings on recitation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المنقول عنه نقلا متواترا .
اور بعض حضرات نے صرف صفت اعجاز اور صفت منزل سے تعریف کی کہ القرآن هو المعجز والمكتوب في المصاحف کیونکہ مقصود تعریف سے یہ ہے کہ اس کے لوازم بعینہ کو ذکر کر دیا جائے اور باقی قیود لوازم میں سے نہیں ہیں
اس لئے کہ حضور ویو کے دور میں قرآن موجود تھا لیکن مکتوب فی المصاحف نہیں تھا اور نقل تو اتر کی ضرورت بھی نہیں تھی۔ اور بعض حضرات نے کہا کہ جو لوگ آپ صلى الله عليه وسلم کے دور میں موجود تھے ان کیلئے تعریف کی ضرورت ہی نہیں ہے کیونکہ وہ تو قرآن کا مشاہدہ کر رہے تھے البتہ بعد میں آنیوالے کیلئے تعریف کی ضرورت ہے اس لئے انہوں نے تین صفات کا لحاظ کیا
(1) المنزل علی الرسول ہو۔
(۲) مکتوب في المصاحف ہو۔
(۳) تواتر کے ساتھ منقول ہو۔
کیونکہ یہ قیودان کے حق میں زیادہ بین ہیں اور واضح ہیں صفت اعجاز سے کیونکہ اعجاز نہ تو لوازم بعینہ میں سے ہے کیونکہ اس کو صرف علماء سمجھتے ہیں عوام نہیں سمجھتے اور نہ ہی لوازم شاملہ میں سے ہے کیونکہ یہ صرف کلام تام میں پایا جاتا ہے قرآن کے ہر ہر جزو میں نہیں پایا جاتا اور مصنف نے جو تعریف کی ہے اس میں صرف دوصفات ذکر کی ہیں کیونکہ مصنف کا مقصود قرآن کو غیر قرآن سے ممتاز کرنا تھا اور وہ ان دو صفات سے ہو جاتا ہے کیونکہ المکتوب فی المصاحف سے احادیث قدسیہ و نبویہ اور دوسری کتب سماویہ خارج ہو جاتی ہیں کیونکہ یہ مکتوب فی المصاحف نہیں اور تواتر کی قید سے قراءت شازہ خارج ہو گئیں کیونکہ وہ بطریق تو اثر منقول نہیں بلکہ بطریق آحاد منقول ہیں وہ جوابی بن کعب کے مصحف کے ساتھ خاص ہیں جیسے انہوں نے قضاء رمضان کے بارے میں یوں قروات نقل کی ہے فعدة من ايام اخر متتابعات اور یا وہ بطریق شہرت منقول ہیں اور وہ حضرة ابن مسعود کے مصحف میں لکھی ہوئی ہیں جیسے کفارہ یمین کے متعلق انہوں نے نقل کیا ثلثۃ ایام متابعات یہ ہے وجہ اختلاف اصولیین کی تعریف کرنے میں۔
Transmitted from him by mutawatir transmission. And some people defined it with only the attribute of inimitability and the attribute of being revealed: that the Quran is the inimitable and written in the mushafs. Because the purpose of definition is to mention its essential properties, and the remaining restrictions are not among the properties. Because in the era of the Prophet (peace be upon him), the Quran existed but was not written in the mushafs, and there was no need for transmission. And some people said that those who were present in the era of the Prophet (peace be upon him) had no need for a definition because they were observing the Quran directly; however, for those who came later, a definition is needed. Therefore, they considered three attributes: (1) It must be revealed to the Messenger, (2) Written in the mushafs, (3) Transmitted with tawatur. Because these restrictions are more clear and evident for them than the attribute of inimitability, because inimitability is not among the essential properties, as only scholars understand it, not the common people, and it is not among the comprehensive properties because it is found only in complete speech, not in every part of the Quran. And the author, in his definition, only mentioned two attributes, because his purpose was to distinguish the Quran from non-Quran, and that is achieved by these two attributes. Because "written in the mushafs" excludes hadith qudsi, prophetic hadith, and other revealed books, as they are not written in the mushafs. And the restriction of tawatur excludes irregular readings, because they are not transmitted by tawatur but by ahad; they are specific to the mushaf of Ubayy ibn Ka'b, such as he narrated regarding making up Ramadan: "fast consecutive days from other days"; or they are transmitted by fame and are written in the mushaf of Ibn Mas'ud, such as he narrated regarding the expiation of an oath: "fast three consecutive days." This is the reason for the disagreement among usuliyyun in defining it.
Source pages p. 435

Commentary
This passage discusses the definition of the Quran and the differences among usul scholars. Some scholars defined the Quran using only two attributes: inimitability (i'jaz) and being revealed. However, later scholars noted that during the Prophet's time, the Quran was not yet written in a single mushaf, so they added the condition of being written in the mushafs and transmitted by tawatur. The author's own definition includes only 'written in the mushafs' and 'mutawatir transmission' because that suffices to distinguish it from other texts. Irregular qira'at (such as those from Ubayy ibn Ka'b or Ibn Mas'ud) are excluded because they are not transmitted mutawatir but by ahad.

Key Terms
القرآن (al-Qur'an): The revealed book, defined by being written in mushafs and transmitted by tawatur
التواتر (al-tawatur): Transmission by a large number of narrators at each level, making collusion on falsehood impossible
المكتوب في المصاحف (al-maktub fi al-masahif): The text that is physically written in the standardized copies of the Quran

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why did some scholars reject the condition of inimitability (i'jaz) in the definition of the Quran?
A) Because it was not needed for those who witnessed the Prophet.
B) Because inimitability is found in complete speech, not every part.
C) Because it was not transmitted by tawatur.
D) Because the author omitted it.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi definition of the Quran as 'written in the mushafs and transmitted by tawatur' effectively excludes irregular readings and other revealed books, providing a clear operational boundary.

Definition of the Quran: Whole vs. Part
Debates the scope of the definition of Quran in usul al-fiqh

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how the Hanafi usul definition of the Quran avoids the fallacy of 'umum al-mushtarak' and apply the concept of 'wad' wahid' to a similar definitional problem.

Core Idea
The Quran is a reality in both whole and part through one assignment, not two—avoiding the homonymy fallacy.

In this passage, the commentator refines the classical definition of the Quran—'what has been transmitted between the covers of the masahif'—to address a critical nuance: does it apply to every letter or only to the complete collection? The resolution hinges on distinguishing the universal meaning (mahiyya) from the personal whole (majmu' shakhsi). Understanding this distinction is essential for usul al-fiqh because it determines how we derive rulings from partial Quranic expressions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core definition and its scope
The definition 'ma naqala ilayna bayna dafatay al-masahif' is examined for whether it includes parts (letters/words) or only the whole.
The commentator begins by raising the issue of generality vs specificity.
2
Recognize the objection
If left general, it includes letters not called Quran; if restricted to complete speech, partial verses are excluded despite Quranic rulings applying.
This is the central tension: the definition must balance inclusivity and typical usage.
3
Understand the author's solution
The definition is for the personal whole (majmu' shakhsi), not a universal predicate. Thus, it applies only to the entire collection, not necessarily to each part individually.
This avoids the need for the definition to cover every single letter.
4
Analyze the response to the homonymy objection
The passage argues that 'Quran' is a reality in both whole and part by a single assignment (wad' wahid), not two separate meanings, thus avoiding umum al-mushtarak.
This is the key theoretical move: one inclusive wad' instead of two.
5
Apply to usul reasoning
This clarifies how terms can cover both whole and part in fiqh rulings (e.g., 'al-kitab' referring to the Quran as a source of law).
The practical outcome: a single verse is a valid dalil without ambiguity.

فَعَلَى مَا هُوَ الْمُنَاسِبُ لِغَرَضِ الْأُصُولِي يَكُونُ الْمُرَادُ بِمَا نُقِلَ إِلَيْنَا بَيْنَ دَفَتَى الْمَصَاحِفِ هُوَ مَا يَشْمَلُ الْكُلَّ وَالْبَعْضَ إِلَّا أَنَّهُ إِنْ أُبْقِيَ عَلَى عُمُومِهِ يَدْخُلُ فِي الحَدّ الحَرْف أَو الكَلِمَةُ مِنْ الْقُرْآنِ وَلَا يُسَمَّى قُرْآنًا فِي عُرْفِ الشَّرْعِ ، وَإِنْ خُصَّ بِالْكَلامِ السَّامُ خَرَجَ بَعْضُ مَا لَيْسَ بِكَلامٍ تَامٌ مَعَ أَنَّهُ يُسَمِّى قُرْآنًا وَيَحْرُمُ مَسُّهُ عَلَى حَدِثِ وَتِلاوَتُهُ عَلَى الْجُنُبِ وَعَلَى مَا دَلَّ عَلَيْهِ سِيَاقُ كَلَامِ الْمُصَنِّفِ الْمُرَادُ بِمَا نُقِلَ مَجْمُوعُ مَا نُقِلَ ؛ لِأَنَّهُ جَعَلَهُ تَعْرِيفًا لِلْمَجُمُوع الشَّخْصِي لَا لِلْمَعْنَى - الكُلّى فَلا يَرِدُ عَلَيْهِ شَيْءٌ إِلَّا أَنَّهُ لَا يُنَاسِبُ غَرَضَ الْأُصُولِي ، فَإِنْ قِيلَ فَالْكِتَابُ بِالْمَعْنَى الثّانِي هَلْ يَصِحُ تَفْسِيرُهُ بِالْقُرْآنِ قُلْنَا نَعَمُ عَلَى أَنْ يَكُونَ الْقُرْآنُ أَيْضًا حَقِيقَةٌ فِي الْبَعْضِ كَمَا هُوَ حَقِيقَةٌ فِي الْكُلِّ ، فَإِنْ قِيلَ فَيَلْزَمُ عُمُومُ الْمُشْتَرَكِ قُلْنَا لَيْسَ مَعْنَى كَوْنِهِ حَقِيقَةٌ فِى الْبَعْضِ كَمَا أَنَّهُ حَقِيقَةٌ فِي الْكُلِّ أَنَّهُ مَوْضُوعٌ لِلْبَعْضِ خاصةً كَمَا أَنَّهُ مَوْضُوعَ لِلكُلِّ خَاصَّةٌ حَتَّى يَكُونَ حَمُلُهُ عَلَى الكُلِّ وَعَلَى الْبَعْضِ مِنْ عُمُومِ المُشْتَرَى ، بَلْ هُوَ مَوْضُوع تَارَةً لِلكُلِّ خَاصَّةً وَتَارَةً لِمَا يَعُمُ الكُل وَالْبَعْضَ أَعْنِي الكَلامَ المَنقُولَ فِى الْمُصْحَفِ تَوَاتُرًا فَيَكُونُ حَقِيقَةٌ فِي الْكُلِّ وَالْبَعْضِ بِاعْتِبَارِ وَضْعِ وَاحِدٍ وَلَا يَكُونُ مِنْ عُمُومِ الْمُشْتَرَكِ فِي شَيْءٍ
ترجمہ:۔ پس اس پر وہ جو مناسب ہے اصولی کی غرض کے وہ یہ ہے کہ جو مراد ما نقل الینا بین دفتی المصاحف سے وہ جو کہ شامل ہو کل کو اور بعض کو مگر بے شک اگر باقی رکھا جائے اس کے عموم پر تو داخل ہو جائیگا حد میں حرف اور کلمہ قرآن میں سے حالانکہ نہیں نام رکھا جاتا اس کا شریعت کے عرف میں قرآن اور اگر خاص کر دیا جائے کلام تام کے ساتھ تو نکل جائیں گی بعض وہ جو کلام نام نہیں ہیں باوجود یکہ ان کا نام قرآن رکھا جاتا ہے اور حرام ہے ان کا چھونا محدث پر اور اس کی تلاوت جنبی پر اور اس پر کہ دلالت کرتا ہے اس پر کلام مصنف کا سیاق اور وہ یہ ہے کہ ہو مراد مانقل سے مجموعہ مانقل کا کیونکہ اس نے بنایا اس کو تعریف مجموعہ شخصی کی نہ کہ معنی کلی کی اور نہیں وارد ہوتا اس پر کوئی اعتراض مگر یہ کہ بیشک نہیں ہے مناسب یہ اصولی کی غرض کے۔ پس اگر اعتراض کیا جائے کہ پس کتاب معنی ثانی کے ساتھ نہیں ہے صحیح ہے اس کی تفسیر قرآن کے ساتھ ہم کہتے ہیں جی ہاں بناء کرتے ہوئے اس پر کہ ہو قرآن حقیقت بعض میں بھی جیسا کہ حقیقت ہے کل میں پس اگر اعتراض کیا جائے کہ لازم آتا ہے عموم مشترک تو ہم کہتے ہیں کہ نہیں ہے معنی اس کے بعض میں حقیقت ہونے کا جیسا کہ وہ حقیقت ہے کل میں کہ وہ وضع کیا گیا ہو بعض کیلئے خاص طور پر جیسا کہ موضوع کل کیلئے خاص طور پرچتی کہ ہو اس کو محمول کرنا اس کو کل پر اور بعض پر عموم مشترک میں سے بلکہ وہ موضوع ہے کبھی کل کیلئے خاص طور پر اور کبھی اس کیلئے جو عام ہے کل کو اور بعض کو مراد لیتا ہوں میں وہ کلام جو منقول ہے مصاحف میں تو اتر کے ساتھ پس ہو گا حقیقت کل اور بعض میں باعتبار ایک وضع کے اور نہیں ہوگا عموم مشترک میں سے کچھ بھی ۔
تشریح فعلی ما حو المناسب :۔ یہ شرح کی عبارت کا چھٹا حصہ ہے فرض شارع ماتن پر اعتراض کرتا ہے لیکن اعتراض سے پہلے ایک مقدمہ سمجھیں۔
اصولیین حروف مبانی اور کلمات غیر دالہ علی المعانی سے بحث نہیں کرتے
مقدمہ:۔ اصولیین اور فقہا حروف مبانی سے بحث نہیں کرتے یعنی ایسے حروف جو کلمات کیلئے بنیاد ہیں ان سے بحث نہیں کرتے اور ان کلمات سے بھی بحث نہیں کرتے جن سے کوئی حکم ثابت نہ ہوتا ہو البتہ ایسے کلمات سے بحث کرتے ہیں جن سے کوئی حکم ثابت ہوتا ہے اور حروف معانی سے بھی بحث کرتے ہیں جیسے غم و اوحتی وغیرہ اسی طرح خاص عام مشترک موال و غیره سے بھی بحث کرتے ہیں البتہ قراء حضرات حروف مہانی سے بھی بحث کرتے ہیں اور کلمات سے بھی بحث کرتے ہیں۔ مانقل الینا الخ میں ما سے مراد وہ الفاظ اور حروف جو احکام یا معانی پر دلالت کریں سوال:۔ یہ جو تعریف کی ہے قرآن کی مانقل الینا الخ یہ تعریف مفہوم کلی کی ہے یعنی جس کا اطلاق الحمد سے لیکر والناس تک کے مجموعہ پر بھی ہو اور بعض پر بھی ہو یایہ تعریف مجموع شخص کی ہے اگرتو یہ تعریف مفہوم کلی کی ہے تو ہم کہیں گے مانقل الینا میں ما
Then according to what is suitable for the purpose of the usuli, the intended meaning of 'what has been transmitted to us between the covers of the masahif' is that which includes the whole and the part, except that if it is left upon its generality, it would include in the definition a letter or a word from the Quran, and it is not called Quran in the custom of the Shariah. And if it is specified with complete speech, some things that are not complete speech would exit, despite the fact that they are called Quran, and it is prohibited to touch it for a person in a state of minor impurity, and to recite it for a junub. And according to what the context of the author's speech indicates, the intended meaning of 'what has been transmitted' is the totality of what has been transmitted, because he made it a definition of the personal whole, not of the universal meaning, so no objection arises against it except that it is not suitable for the purpose of the usuli. So if it is said: 'Then Kitab in the second meaning, is it correct to interpret it as Quran?' We say: Yes, provided that Quran is also a reality in the part just as it is a reality in the whole. So if it is said: 'Then the commonality of the homonym follows.' We say: The meaning of it being a reality in the part just as it is a reality in the whole is not that it is assigned specifically to the part just as it is assigned specifically to the whole, such that applying it to the whole and to the part would be from the commonality of the homonym. Rather, it is assigned sometimes specifically to the whole, and sometimes to that which includes the whole and the part, I mean the speech transmitted in the mus-haf by tawatur. So it is a reality in the whole and the part by virtue of one assignment, and it is not from the commonality of the homonym at all.
Source pages p. 439

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on a matn definition of the Quran, specifically the phrase 'ma naqala ilayna bayna dafatay al-masahif' (what has been transmitted to us between the covers of the masahif). The commentator addresses an objection: if the definition is taken generally, it includes letters and words that are not considered Quran in the custom of the Shariah. If it is restricted to complete speech (kalam tam), then partial verses (like a single word) would be excluded, yet they are indeed called Quran and rulings such as prohibition of touching by a person in minor impurity and recitation by a junub apply. The commentator then explains that the author's intention is to define the Quran as a 'personal whole' (majmu' shakhsi) — i.e., the entire collection — not as a universal meaning that applies to each part. Thus, the definition does not need to apply to each individual letter or word. The passage then discusses whether 'kitab' (in its second meaning) can be interpreted as 'Quran', and responds that it can, provided it is understood that Quran is a reality in the part as well, but not through homonymy (ishtirak) — rather, it is assigned both to the whole and to the inclusive whole-part meaning via a single assignment. The Urdu section adds a preliminary note that usul scholars do not discuss letters of construction (huruf al-mabani) or words that do not convey a ruling; they only discuss letters of meaning and words that convey rulings. The final part raises the question whether the definition is for the universal meaning or the personal whole, but the text cuts off.

Key Terms
ما نقل إلينا بين دفتي المصاحف (ma naqala ilayna bayna dafatay al-masahif): The phrase used to define the Quran: that which has been transmitted to us between the covers of the masahif (copies of the Quran).
المجموع الشخصي (al-majmu' al-shakhsi): The personal whole, i.e., the entire collection of the Quran as a single entity, not as a universal predicable.
حقيقة في الكل والبعض (haqiqah fi al-kull wa al-ba'd): A term that is a reality (literal) for both the whole and the part, but not through homonymy; rather, it is assigned to both by a single assignment.
عموم المشترك (umum al-mushtarak): The fallacy of using a homonym in both of its meanings simultaneously, which is invalid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does defining the Quran as 'ma naqala ilayna' not lead to 'umum al-mushtarak'?
A) Because the definition excludes letters and words.
B) Because the term is assigned to both whole and part by a single wad'.
C) Because the author uses it only for the personal whole.
D) Because usul scholars do not consider the issue.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi definition of the Quran as a personal whole, and the concept of a single assignment covering both whole and part, prevents the fallacy of umum al-mushtarak.

Al-Quran as Source
intermediate
12 min
Definition of the Quran and Its Scope in Hanafi Usul al-Fiqh
Defining the Quran and defending its scope

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi definition of the Quran, classify its scope (universal meaning vs. specific collection), and apply the definition to distinguish what is considered Quran from what is not.

Core Idea
The Quran is defined as transmitted speech indicating rulings or meanings, whether as a whole or in part, excluding letters and meaningless words.

This passage from a commentary on al-Tawdih wa al-Talwih explores the precise definition of the Quran in Hanafi usul. It addresses key questions: Does the definition cover the entire collection or also individual verses? What exactly does 'ma' (what) in the definition refer to? The commentator resolves these issues by showing that the definition is flexible enough to serve the usuli's purpose while remaining precise.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition and its key components
Locate the definition: 'ma nuqila ilayna' (that which is transmitted to us). Note the premise about huruf al-mabani and words without rulings. Understand that the definition excludes letters and meaningless words.
The definition is in the first part of the Arabic text.
2
Analyze the scope of 'ma' in the definition
Consider the three possibilities for 'ma': general (complete/incomplete speech), complete speech only, or words indicating rulings/meanings. Evaluate each against the usuli's purpose.
The commentator argues that 'ma' should refer to words indicating rulings or meanings.
3
Distinguish between universal meaning and specific collection
Determine whether the definition is for the universal meaning (mafhum kulli) applying to whole and parts, or the specific collection (majmu shakhsi) as a single entity. Both are possible according to the text.
If universal meaning, the definition excludes huruf al-mabani. If specific collection, it still serves the usuli because he discusses verses as parts of the collection.
4
Resolve the objection about tafsir of 'kitab' by 'Quran'
Address the concern that 'kitab' (universal) cannot be interpreted as 'Quran' (specific collection) because that would be tafsir bil-asha'ib (explaining broader by narrower). The response is that 'Quran' also applies to parts, so no contradiction.
This shows the definition's flexibility: the Quran is a reality in both whole and parts.

نام رکھا جاتا اس کا شریعت کے عرف میں قرآن اور اگر خاص کر دیا جائے کلام تام کے ساتھ تو نکل جائیں گی بعض وہ جو کلام نام نہیں ہیں باوجود یکہ ان کا نام قرآن رکھا جاتا ہے اور حرام ہے ان کا چھونا محدث پر اور اس کی تلاوت جنبی پر اور اس پر کہ دلالت کرتا ہے اس پر کلام مصنف کا سیاق اور وہ یہ ہے کہ ہو مراد مانقل سے مجموعہ مانقل کا کیونکہ اس نے بنایا اس کو تعریف مجموعہ شخصی کی نہ کہ معنی کلی کی اور نہیں وارد ہوتا اس پر کوئی اعتراض مگر یہ کہ بیشک نہیں ہے مناسب یہ اصولی کی غرض کے۔ پس اگر اعتراض کیا جائے کہ پس کتاب معنی ثانی کے ساتھ نہیں ہے صحیح ہے اس کی تفسیر قرآن کے ساتھ ہم کہتے ہیں جی ہاں بناء کرتے ہوئے اس پر کہ ہو قرآن حقیقت بعض میں بھی جیسا کہ حقیقت ہے کل میں پس اگر اعتراض کیا جائے کہ لازم آتا ہے عموم مشترک تو ہم کہتے ہیں کہ نہیں ہے معنی اس کے بعض میں حقیقت ہونے کا جیسا کہ وہ حقیقت ہے کل میں کہ وہ وضع کیا گیا ہو بعض کیلئے خاص طور پر جیسا کہ موضوع کل کیلئے خاص طور پرچتی کہ ہو اس کو محمول کرنا اس کو کل پر اور بعض پر عموم مشترک میں سے بلکہ وہ موضوع ہے کبھی کل کیلئے خاص طور پر اور کبھی اس کیلئے جو عام ہے کل کو اور بعض کو مراد لیتا ہوں میں وہ کلام جو منقول ہے مصاحف میں تو اتر کے ساتھ پس ہو گا حقیقت کل اور بعض میں باعتبار ایک وضع کے اور نہیں ہوگا عموم مشترک میں سے کچھ بھی ۔
تشریح فعلی ما حو المناسب :۔ یہ شرح کی عبارت کا چھٹا حصہ ہے فرض شارع ماتن پر اعتراض کرتا ہے لیکن اعتراض سے پہلے ایک مقدمہ سمجھیں۔
اصولییین حروف مبانی اور کلمات غیر دالہ علی المعانی سے بحث نہیں کرتے
مقدمہ:۔ اصولیین اور فقہا حروف مبانی سے بحث نہیں کرتے یعنی ایسے حروف جو کلمات کیلئے بنیاد ہیں ان سے بحث نہیں کرتے اور ان کلمات سے بھی بحث نہیں کرتے جن سے کوئی حکم ثابت نہ ہوتا ہو البتہ ایسے کلمات سے بحث کرتے ہیں جن سے کوئی حکم ثابت ہوتا ہے اور حروف معانی سے بھی بحث کرتے ہیں جیسے غم و اوحتی وغیرہ اسی طرح خاص عام مشترک موال و غیره
سے بھی بحث کرتے ہیں البتہ قراء حضرات حروف مہانی سے بھی بحث کرتے ہیں اور کلمات سے بھی بحث کرتے ہیں۔ مانقل الینا الخ میں ما سے مراد وہ الفاظ اور حروف جو احکام یا معانی پر دلالت کریں سوال:۔ یہ جو تعریف کی ہے قرآن کی مانقل الینا الخ یہ تعریف مفہوم کلی کی ہے یعنی جس کا اطلاق الحمد سے لیکر والناس تک کے مجموعہ پر بھی ہو اور بعض پر بھی ہو یایہ تعریف مجموع شخص کی ہے اگرتو یہ تعریف مفہوم کلی کی ہے تو ہم کہیں گے مانقل الینا میں ما
سے کیا مراد ہے؟ اس سے مراد عام ہے کلام تام ہو یا غیر تام ہو یا اس سے مراد صرف کلام نام ہے؟ اگر آپ کہیں کہ ما سے مراد عام ہے کہ کلام تام ہو یا غیر نام تو پھر یہ تعریف حروف مبانی اور ان کلمات پر بھی صادق آئیگی جن سے حکم ثابت نہیں ہوتا تو ایسی صورت میں حروف مبانی کا قرآن میں داخل ہونا لازم آئیگا حالانکہ عرف اصولیین میں حروف مبانی قرآن نہیں ہیں اور وہ کلمات بھی قرآن میں داخل ہوں گے جن سے حکم ثابت نہیں ہوتا حالانکہ عرف اصولیین میں وہ بھی قرآن نہیں ہیں اور اگر ما سے مراد کلام تام ہو تو پھر کلام غیر نام قرآن سے خارج ہوگا حالانکہ کلام غیر نام سے اصولی بحث کرتے ہیں وہ ایسے کہ بعض آیات تام نہیں ہیں لیکن محدث کا ان کو چھونا جائز نہیں ہے تو ایسی صورت میں یہ قرآن سے خارج ہوں گی حالانکہ عرف شرع میں قرآن ہے اور اگر یہ تعریف مانقل الینا والی مجموعہ شخص کی ہو جیسے ماتن" کے سیاق سے معلوم ہوتا ۔ ہم پھر اعتراض یہ ہے کہ یہ اصولی کی غرض کے خلاف ہے کیونکہ اصولی مجموعہ سے بحث نہیں کرتا وہ آیت آیت سے بحث کرتا ہے کل سے بحث نہیں کرتا تو
اب کیا مراد ہے؟
جواب:۔ یہ ہے کہ دونوں احتمال مراد لئے جاسکتے ہیں یعنی مفہوم کلی اور مجموع مشخصی دونوں احتمال درست ہیں یعنی دونوں کی تعریف ہو سکتی ہے تو اگر مفہوم کلی کی تعریف ہو تو ما سے مراد نہ تو کلام تام ہے اور نہ عام مراد ہے کہ کلام تام ہو یا غیر تام ہو بلکہ ما سے مراد وہ الفاظ ہوں گے جو حکم پر اور معنی پر دلالت کرتے ہیں لہذا اب اعتراض نہیں ہوگا کیونکہ اصولی ان سے بحث کرتے ہیں چاہے فعل ہوں اسم ہوں حرف ہوں اور وہ کلمات جو معانی یا احکام پر دلالت نہیں کرتے اور حروف مبانی وہ خارج ہوں گے تو یہ تعریف جامع مانع ہوگی اور اگر مجموعہ شخص کی تعریف ہو تو جواب یہ ہے کہ پھر بھی اصولی کی غرض کے خلاف نہیں ہے کیونکہ جب اصولی آیت آیت سے بحث کرتا ہے تو اس حیثیت سے بحث کرتا ہے کہ یہ مجموعہ کا حصہ ہے لہذا یہ اصولی کی فرض کے خلاف نہیں۔
کیا کتاب بمعنی مفہوم کلی کی تفسیر قرآن سے جائز ہے
فان تیل :۔ یہ شرح کی عبارت کا ساتواں حصہ ہے فرض شارع سوال ذکر کر کے اس کا جواب دیتا ہے۔ اعتراض:- یہ ہے کہ اگر کتاب سے مراد معنی کلی ہو کہ جس کا اطلاق مجموعہ اور بعض پر ہوتا ہے تو پھر لفظ کتاب کی تفسیر لفظ قرآن سے جائز نہ ہوگی مثلا الکتاب ای القرآن کہنا جائز نہ ہوگا کیونکہ بظاہر تو یہ تعریف نہیں بن سکتا ہے اس لئے کہ قرآن کا اطلاق صرف مجموعہ شخص پر ہوتا ہے جو الحمد سے شروع ہو کر والناس پر ختم ہوتا ہے اور کتاب (معرف) بمعنی کلی ہے اس کا اطلاق کل پر بھی ہے اور بعض پر بھی ہے تو ان کے درمیان تو منافات سمجھ میں آرہی ہے لہدایہ تفسیر بالسبائین ہوئی اور تفسیر بالسباین جائز نہیں ہوتی ۔
It is named as Quran in the custom of Sharia. And if it is specified with complete speech, then some of those which are not called speech will come out, although they are named Quran, and touching them is forbidden for a hadith-major person, and reciting it for a junub. And the context of the author's speech indicates that what is meant by 'ma nuqila' is the transmitted collection, because he made it a definition of a specific collection, not of a universal meaning. And no objection occurs upon it except that it is not suitable for the purpose of the usuli. So if it is objected: then 'kitab' with the second meaning is not valid; its tafsir with Quran; we say: yes, building upon that the Quran is a reality in some parts just as it is a reality in the whole. So if it is objected: it necessitates common usage (ishtirak); we say: there is no meaning of its being a reality in some parts like it is a reality in the whole, that it was placed for some parts specifically, just as it is placed for the whole specifically, such that applying it to the whole and to some parts would be from common usage. Rather, it is placed sometimes for the whole specifically and sometimes for that which is general, intending the whole and some parts. I mean that speech which is transmitted in the musahif, with its descent, then it will be a reality in the whole and in some parts by virtue of one placement, and it will not be anything from common usage. Practical explanation of what is appropriate: This is the sixth part of the commentary's expression; the commentator assumes that the author objects, but before the objection understand a premise. The usuliyyun do not discuss letters of construction (huruf al-mabani) and words that do not indicate meanings. Premise: The usuliyyun and fuqaha do not discuss huruf al-mabani, i.e., letters which are the basis for words; they do not discuss them, and they do not discuss those words from which no ruling is established. Rather, they discuss words from which a ruling is established, and they discuss huruf al-ma'ani (letters of meaning) like 'gham' and 'aw' etc. Similarly, they discuss khas, amm, mushtarak, mawal, etc. However, the qurra' (reciters) do discuss huruf al-mabani and also words. In 'ma nuqila ilayna' etc., what is meant by 'ma' are those words and letters which indicate rulings or meanings. Question: This definition of the Quran, 'ma nuqila ilayna' etc., is it a definition of a universal meaning (mafhum kulli), i.e., its application is to the whole collection from al-Hamd to al-Nas, and also to some parts? Or is it a definition of a specific collection (majmu shakhsi)? If it is a definition of a universal meaning, then we say: what is meant by 'ma' in 'ma nuqila ilayna'? Does it mean general, whether complete speech or incomplete speech? Or does it mean only speech? If you say 'ma' means general, whether complete speech or incomplete speech, then this definition would apply to huruf al-mabani and those words from which no ruling is established, so in that case it would necessitate including huruf al-mabani in the Quran, whereas in the custom of usuliyyun, huruf al-mabani are not Quran, and those words from which no ruling is established would also be included in the Quran, whereas in the custom of usuliyyun they are not Quran. And if 'ma' means complete speech, then incomplete speech would be excluded from the Quran, whereas the usuli discusses incomplete speech, such as some verses that are not complete, but touching them is not permissible for a hadith-major person, so in that case they would be excluded from the Quran, whereas in the custom of Sharia they are Quran. And if this definition of 'ma nuqila ilayna' is for a specific collection, as appears from the context of the author, then we have the objection that it is contrary to the purpose of the usuli, because the usuli does not discuss the collection; he discusses verse by verse, not the whole. So what is meant? Answer: It is that both probabilities can be intended, i.e., both the universal meaning and the specific collection are possible, i.e., both can be the definition. So if it is a definition of the universal meaning, then what is meant by 'ma' is neither complete speech nor general, i.e., not that it means complete or incomplete speech. Rather, what is meant by 'ma' are those words that indicate a ruling and a meaning. Therefore, the objection does not arise, because the usuli discusses these, whether they are verbs, nouns, or particles. And those words that do not indicate meanings or rulings, and huruf al-mabani, they will be excluded. So this definition will be comprehensive and exclusive. And if it is a definition of the specific collection, the answer is that it is not contrary to the usuli's purpose, because when the usuli discusses verse by verse, he discusses them as parts of the collection. Therefore, this is not against his purpose. Is the tafsir of 'kitab' in the sense of universal meaning with 'Quran' permissible? Fan tiyl: This is the seventh part of the commentary's expression; the commentator presents a question and answers it. Objection: If by 'kitab' is meant the universal meaning, i.e., that is applied to the whole and to some parts, then the tafsir of the word 'kitab' with the word 'Quran' would not be permissible, e.g., saying 'al-kitab ay al-Quran' would not be permissible, because apparently it cannot be a definition, since the application of 'Quran' is only to the specific collection that begins with al-Hamd and ends with al-Nas, while 'kitab' (with the definite article) is in the universal meaning, its application is to the whole and to some parts, so there is a contradiction between them; hence this would be a tafsir of something broader by something narrower (tafsir bil-asha'ib), and tafsir bil-asha'ib is not permissible.
Source pages p. 440

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of the definition of the Quran in Hanafi usul. It begins by stating that the Quran is named as such in Sharia, and if the definition were restricted to complete speech (kalam tam), some things that are not considered speech would be excluded, yet they are called Quran and have rulings like prohibition of touching for a person in minor impurity. The author's context indicates that the definition is for the specific transmitted collection (majmu shakhsi), not a universal meaning. An objection is raised that this definition is not suitable for the usuli's purpose. The commentator then sets a premise: usuliyyun do not discuss huruf al-mabani (letters of the alphabet) or words that do not indicate rulings; they discuss only words that indicate rulings or meanings. The word 'ma' in the definition is analyzed. The commentator considers whether 'ma' refers to general (including complete and incomplete speech), or only to complete speech, or only to words indicating rulings. Each possibility is examined: if general, the definition would include letters and meaningless words, which are not considered Quran by usuliyyun; if only complete speech, incomplete verses would be excluded, though they are Quran in Sharia. Then the question is asked: is the definition for universal meaning (mafhum kulli) or specific collection? Both are possible. If universal meaning, then 'ma' refers to words that indicate rulings and meanings, which is what usuliyyun discuss, so the definition is comprehensive and exclusive. If specific collection, the usuli discusses verses as parts of the collection, so it's still within his scope. Finally, the question of whether 'kitab' (universal) can be interpreted as 'Quran' (specific) is addressed. The objection is that 'kitab' is general and 'Quran' is specific to the whole, so the interpretation would be tafsir bil-asha'ib (explaining the broader by the narrower), which is invalid. But the answer is that 'Quran' is also used for parts, not just the whole, so it is not a problem. The commentator maintains that both definitions are valid and the objections are resolved.

Key Terms
ما (ma): In the definition 'ma nuqila ilayna', it refers to words that indicate rulings or meanings, excluding letters and words without rulings.
حروف مبانی (huruf al-mabani): Letters of the alphabet that are the building blocks of words, not discussed by usuliyyun.
مفہوم کلی (mafhum kulli): A universal meaning that applies to both the whole collection and to individual parts.
مجموع شخصی (majmu shakhsi): A specific collection considered as a single entity (the Qur'an as a whole from al-Fatiha to al-Nas).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi usul discussion in this passage, why is the definition of the Quran as 'ma nuqila ilayna' considered suitable for the usuli's purpose even if it means the specific collection (majmu shakhsi)?
A) Because the usuli only discusses the entire Quran as a whole.
B) Because the usuli discusses individual verses as parts of the collection, so it is still within his scope.
C) Because the definition excludes huruf al-mabani.
D) Because the usuli does not discuss verses at all.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi definition of the Quran as 'that which is transmitted to us' is flexible enough to cover both the entire collection and individual verses, excluding only letters and meaningless words, and thus serves the usuli's purpose of deriving rulings from specific Quranic expressions.

Resolving the Meaning of 'Ma' in the Definition of the Quran
Resolving the ambiguity of 'ma' in the Quran's definition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze the ambiguity of the word 'ma' in the usul definition of the Quran and apply the correct interpretation depending on whether we mean the general concept or the specific compilation.

Core Idea
The word 'ma' in the Quran's definition can mean either the general concept (words indicating rulings) or the specific mushaf — both are valid, each answering different objections.

The definition of the Quran in usul al-fiqh is 'what is transmitted to us.' But what does 'what' refer to? This passage explores the ambiguity of the word 'ma' and shows how the Hanafi understanding resolves potential contradictions, ensuring the definition is both comprehensive and exclusive.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ambiguous term
In the definition 'al-munqal ilayna' (what is transmitted to us), the word 'ma' (what) is ambiguous. Does it refer to anything (general), only complete speech, or something else?
2
Test the 'general' interpretation
If 'ma' is general, the definition includes everything: letters (huruf mabani) and words without rulings. In usul, those are not Quran, so the definition becomes too broad.
3
Test the 'complete speech' interpretation
If 'ma' means only complete speech, then incomplete verses (e.g., 'Bismillah') are excluded. But they are considered Quran in Shariah, so the definition becomes too narrow.
4
Apply the commentator's resolution
The resolution distinguishes two definitions: mafhum kulli (general concept) and majmu shakhsi (specific compilation). For the general concept, 'ma' refers to words indicating rulings and meanings; for the specific compilation, 'ma' refers to the entire mushaf. Thus both objections are answered.
This makes the definition jami' mani' in both cases.

سے کیا مراد ہے؟ اس سے مراد عام ہے کلام تام ہو یا غیر تام ہو یا اس سے مراد صرف کلام نام ہے؟ اگر آپ کہیں کہ ما سے مراد عام ہے کہ کلام تام ہو یا غیر نام تو پھر یہ تعریف حروف مبانی اور ان کلمات پر بھی صادق آئیگی جن سے حکم ثابت نہیں ہوتا تو ایسی صورت میں حروف مبانی کا قرآن میں داخل ہونا لازم آئیگا حالانکہ عرف اصولیین میں حروف مبانی قرآن نہیں ہیں اور وہ کلمات بھی قرآن میں داخل ہوں گے جن سے حکم ثابت نہیں ہوتا حالانکہ عرف اصولیین میں وہ بھی قرآن نہیں ہیں اور اگر ما سے مراد کلام تام ہو تو پھر کلام غیر نام قرآن سے خارج ہوگا حالانکہ کلام غیر نام سے اصولی بحث کرتے ہیں وہ ایسے کہ بعض آیات تام نہیں ہیں لیکن محدث کا ان کو چھونا جائز نہیں ہے تو ایسی صورت میں یہ قرآن سے خارج ہوں گی حالانکہ عرف شرع میں قرآن ہے اور اگر یہ تعریف مانقل الینا والی مجموعہ شخص کی ہو جیسے ماتن" کے سیاق سے معلوم ہوتا ۔ ہم پھر اعتراض یہ ہے کہ یہ اصولی کی غرض کے خلاف ہے کیونکہ اصولی مجموعہ سے بحث نہیں کرتا وہ آیت آیت سے بحث کرتا ہے کل سے بحث نہیں کرتا تو اب کیا مراد ہے؟ جواب:۔ یہ ہے کہ دونوں احتمال مراد لئے جاسکتے ہیں یعنی مفہوم کلی اور مجموع مشخصی دونوں احتمال درست ہیں یعنی دونوں کی تعریف ہو سکتی ہے تو اگر مفہوم کلی کی تعریف ہو تو ما سے مراد نہ تو کلام تام ہے اور نہ عام مراد ہے کہ کلام تام ہو یا غیر تام ہو بلکہ ما سے مراد وہ الفاظ ہوں گے جو حکم پر اور معنی پر دلالت کرتے ہیں لہذا اب اعتراض نہیں ہوگا کیونکہ اصولی ان سے بحث کرتے ہیں چاہے فعل ہوں اسم ہوں حرف ہوں اور وہ کلمات جو معانی یا احکام پر دلالت نہیں کرتے اور حروف مبانی وہ خارج ہوں گے تو یہ تعریف جامع مانع ہوگی اور اگر مجموعہ شخص کی تعریف ہو تو جواب یہ ہے کہ پھر بھی اصولی کی غرض کے خلاف نہیں ہے کیونکہ جب اصولی آیت آیت سے بحث کرتا ہے تو اس حیثیت سے بحث کرتا ہے کہ یہ مجموعہ کا حصہ ہے لہذا یہ اصولی کی فرض کے خلاف نہیں۔
What is meant by 'ma'? Is it general, including complete speech or incomplete speech, or does 'ma' only mean complete speech? If you say 'ma' is general, including complete or incomplete speech, then this definition would also apply to letters (huruf mabani) and words from which no ruling is derived, which would necessitate including those in the Quran, whereas in the terminology of usuliyyin, letters are not Quran, and those words from which no ruling is derived are also not Quran. And if 'ma' means only complete speech, then incomplete speech is excluded from the Quran, whereas usuliyyin discuss incomplete speech, such as some verses that are not complete, and it is not permissible for a non-mu'min to touch them; so those would be excluded from the Quran, whereas according to the custom of Shariah they are Quran. And if this definition 'what is transmitted to us' refers to the specific compilation, as the context of the matn indicates, then there is an objection that this is against the purpose of usul, because the usuli does not discuss the compilation as a whole; he discusses verse by verse, not the whole. So what is meant? Answer: Both probabilities can be taken, i.e., the general concept and the specific compilation; both are valid, i.e., both definitions are possible. So if it is a definition of the general concept, then 'ma' does not mean complete speech nor general (including complete and incomplete), rather 'ma' means the words that indicate a ruling and a meaning. Hence there is no objection because the usuli discusses these, whether they are verbs, nouns, or particles. Those words that do not indicate meanings or rulings, and letters, will be excluded. So this definition will be comprehensive and exclusive. And if it is a definition of the specific compilation, the answer is that it is still not against the purpose of usul because when the usuli discusses verse by verse, he does so considering them as part of the compilation. Therefore it is not against his objective.
Source pages p. 441

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the definition of the Quran. The author examines the ambiguity of the word 'ma' (that which) in the standard definition: 'what is transmitted to us (al-munqal ilayna)'. The discussion starts by posing the question: does 'ma' refer to anything (general), including complete and incomplete speech, or only to complete speech? Each option is tested against the usul terminology and Shariah custom. If 'ma' is general, the definition would incorrectly include letters (like individual Arabic letters) and words that do not convey rulings, which are not considered Quran in usul. If 'ma' is restricted to complete speech, then incomplete speech (like some verses that are syntactically incomplete) would be excluded, even though they are still considered Quran for ritual purposes. A further objection is raised: if the definition is meant for the specific compilation (the mushaf), then discussing individual verses is not equivalent to discussing the whole, so the definition seems irrelevant to usul. The resolution provided by the commentator is that both interpretations are acceptable depending on whether we are defining the general concept (mafhum kulli) or the specific compilation (majmu shakhsi). For the general concept, 'ma' refers to words that indicate rulings and meanings, which excludes letters and meaningless words. For the specific compilation, the usuli's discussion of individual verses is valid because each verse is a part of that compilation. Thus the definition is both comprehensive (jami') and exclusive (mani') in either case. The commentator thus defends the usul definition against various criticisms.

Key Terms
ما (ma): The relative pronoun in the definition of Quran, interpreted either as general (including complete/incomplete speech) or as specific (only words indicating rulings/meanings).
کلام تام (kalam tam): Complete speech that conveys a full meaning independently.
حروف مبانی (huruf mabani): Individual letters of the alphabet, which are not considered part of the Quran in usul terminology.
مفہوم کلی (mafhum kulli): The general concept or universal meaning abstracted from particular instances.
مجموع شخصی (majmu shakhsi): The specific compilation of the Quran (mushaf) as a concrete individual entity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The definition becomes exclusive.
B) The definition would incorrectly include letters and words that do not indicate rulings.
C) The definition applies only to complete verses.
D) It creates circularity in defining the mushaf.

Key Takeaway
The definition of the Quran as 'what is transmitted to us' is made precise by interpreting 'ma' according to whether we mean the general concept (words conveying rulings) or the specific compilation (the mushaf).

Permissibility of Interpreting 'Kitab' as 'Quran'
Defending the interpretation of 'kitab' as 'quran' against an objection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the permissibility of defining 'kitab' as 'quran' and identify the linguistic reasoning that resolves the objection of tafsir bil-aks.

Core Idea
Both 'kitab' and 'quran' have general and specific meanings; as synonyms in the general sense, defining one by the other is valid.

In this passage, the commentator addresses an objection to a common definition: 'al-kitab ay al-quran'. The objection claims that since 'quran' refers only to the complete compilation, defining the general 'kitab' by the specific 'quran' would be an invalid broader definition. The Hanafi answer reveals that 'quran' itself has a general meaning, making the two terms synonymous and the definition perfectly valid. This lesson explores how recognizing multiple semantic layers prevents logical pitfalls in usul definitions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
What is the opponent's claim about the term 'quran'? How does it lead to tafsir bil-aks?
The opponent restricts 'quran' to the complete compilation only.
2
Examine the Hanafi response
How does the commentator show that 'quran' is not restricted to the complete compilation? What two meanings does it have?
Both 'kitab' and 'quran' have general and specific meanings.
3
Apply the principle of synonymy
Why does tafsir bil-mutaradif (definition by synonym) become permissible once the meanings are aligned?
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
What practical difference does this dispute make?
The definition 'al-kitab huwa al-quran' is valid in Hanafi usul.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
کیا کتاب بمعنی مفہوم کلی کی تفسیر قرآن سے جائز ہے فان تیل :۔ یہ شرح کی عبارت کا ساتواں حصہ ہے فرض شارع سوال ذکر کر کے اس کا جواب دیتا ہے۔ اعتراض:- یہ ہے کہ اگر کتاب سے مراد معنی کلی ہو کہ جس کا اطلاق مجموعہ اور بعض پر ہوتا ہے تو پھر لفظ کتاب کی تفسیر لفظ قرآن سے جائز نہ ہوگی مثلا الکتاب ای القرآن کہنا جائز نہ ہوگا کیونکہ بظاہر تو یہ تعریف نہیں بن سکتا ہے اس لئے کہ قرآن کا اطلاق صرف مجموعہ شخص پر ہوتا ہے جو الحمد سے شروع ہو کر والناس پر ختم ہوتا ہے اور کتاب (معرف) بمعنی کلی ہے اس کا اطلاق کل پر بھی ہے اور بعض پر بھی ہے تو ان کے درمیان تو منافات سمجھ میں آرہی ہے لہدایہ تفسیر بالسبائین ہوئی اور تفسیر بالسباین جائز نہیں ہوتی ۔ جواب:۔ جیسے کتاب کے دو معنی ہیں (۱) بمعنی کلی (۲) بمعنی مجموعه شخصی اس طرح قرآن کے بھی دو معنی ہیں (۱) قرآن بمعنی مفہوم کلی (۲) قرآن بمعنی مجموعه شخصی تو جب معرف (کتاب) کو بمعنی مفہوم کلی لیا جائیگا تو قرآن بھی بمعنی کلی مراد لیا جائیگا خلاصہ یہ ہے کہ جب قرآن کا اطلاق کل میں بھی حقیقت ہے بعض میں بھی حقیقت ہے تو کتاب کی تفسیر قرآن سے درست ہے کیونکہ دونوں ایک دوسرے کے مترادف ہیں اور تفسیر بالمرادف جائز ہوتی ہے۔
Is it permissible to interpret 'kitab' in the sense of general concept as 'quran'? This is the seventh part of the commentary's phrase: the commentator assumed a question and answers it. Objection: If by 'kitab' is meant the general meaning which applies to both the whole and a part, then it is not permissible to interpret the word 'kitab' with the word 'quran', for example, saying 'al-kitab ay al-quran' is not permissible, because apparently this cannot be a definition since 'quran' applies only to the specific compilation starting from al-Hamd to al-Nas, while 'kitab' (the defined) is general, applying to the whole and a part. So there is a contradiction, leading to definition by something broader (tafsir bil-aks) and definition by something broader is not permissible. Answer: Just as 'kitab' has two meanings: (1) general concept, (2) specific compilation, similarly 'quran' also has two meanings: (1) general concept, (2) specific compilation. So when the defined (kitab) is taken in the sense of general concept, then 'quran' is also taken in the general sense. In summary, since 'quran' is used truly for both the whole and a part, interpreting 'kitab' as 'quran' is valid because they are synonymous, and definition by synonym is permissible.
Source pages p. 441

Commentary
This passage addresses an objection to the common practice of explaining 'al-kitab' as 'al-quran'. The objection holds that 'kitab' in its general sense (applying to both the whole and parts) cannot be defined as 'quran' because 'quran' is taken to refer solely to the complete compilation (from al-Fatihah to al-Nas). This would make the definition broader (tafsir bil-aks), which is invalid. The commentator responds by noting that 'quran', like 'kitab', has two meanings: it can refer to the general concept (the divine speech as a universal) or to the specific compilation. Thus when 'kitab' is understood as the general concept, 'quran' in the definition is also taken in its general sense, making them synonyms. Since definition by synonym is permissible, the objection falls. This clarifies that the terms are used with multiple layers of meaning, and one must match the appropriate sense.

Key Terms
کتاب (kitab): The term being defined; can mean either the general concept of divine speech or the specific compilation (mushaf).
قرآن (quran): The term used to define 'kitab'; also has two meanings: general concept and specific compilation.
تفسیر بالمرادف (tafsir bil-mutaradif): Defining a term by a synonym, which is considered permissible in this context.
تفسیر بالسبائین (tafsir bil-aks (or bil-asba'in)): Defining a term by something broader or narrower, which is invalid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Both terms have a general meaning that covers the whole and parts, so they are synonymous when used in that sense.
C) The objection is irrelevant because no one takes 'quran' generally.
D) 'Quran' is broader than 'kitab', so definition by broader is allowed.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that 'quran', like 'kitab', has both general and specific meanings, allowing valid definition by synonym.

Defining Quran for Legal Purposes: Eternal vs. Created Speech
Expansion on the definition of Quran for usul

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the eternal and created aspects of the Quran and explain why the term 'Quran' in usul al-fiqh is defined as the created verbal expression for legal purposes.

Core Idea
The Quran in usul al-fiqh refers to the created verbal speech, because legal rulings attach to it, not to the eternal attribute.

In this passage, the commentator expands on the definition of the Quran as used in usul al-fiqh. He distinguishes between the eternal divine speech, which is an uncreated attribute, and the created verbal expression that we recite. This distinction is crucial because legal rulings (ahkam) are based on the verbal Quran, not on the eternal attribute. The definition, therefore, must include characteristics that specify the verbal Quran, ensuring co-extensiveness with the defined term.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two senses of 'Quran'
The passage distinguishes between eternal speech (al-kalam al-azali) and created verbal speech (al-kalam al-lafzi al-hadith).
Eternal speech is an attribute; verbal speech is composed of sounds and letters.
2
Note the dalil for the eternal sense
The hadith 'The Quran is the speech of Allah, uncreated' establishes the eternal attribute.
This hadith is ahad (zanni al-thubut) but its meaning is clear.
3
Understand the usuli's perspective
Legal rulings are attached to the verbal speech, so in usul the term Quran is defined as the verbal expression.
Rulings like prayer and recitation depend on the audible/textual Quran.
4
Recognize the requirement of musawah
The definition must be co-extensive with the defined; thus additional restrictions (quyud) are needed to precisely specify the verbal Quran.
Merely mentioning 'transmitted' would be insufficient; the definition must exclude other transmitted texts like hadith.

قَوْلُهُ ) بَلْ تَشْخِيصُهُ أَى تَمْيِيرُهُ بِخَوَاصٌهِ فَإِنْ كَلِمَةَ أَي إِنَّمَا يُطْلَبُ بِهَا تَمْييرُ الشَّيْء بِمَا يَخُصُّهُ شَخُصًا كَانَ أَوْ غَيْرَهُ . قَوْلُهُ ) يُطْلَقُ عَلَى الْكَلَامِ الْأَزَلِيٌّ ( كَمَا فِي قَوْلِهِ عَلَيْهِ السَّلَامُ ( الْقُرْآنُ كَلَامُ اللَّهِ تَعَالَى غَيْرٌ مَخْلُوقِ ) الْحَدِيث ، وَهُوَ صِفَةٌ قَدِيمَةٌ مُنَافِيَةٌ لِلسُّكُوتِ وَالْآفَةِ لَيْسَتْ مِنْ جِنسِ الْحُرُوفِ وَالْأَصْوَاتِ لَا تَخْتَلِفُ إِلَى الْأَمْرِ وَالنَّهْي وَالْإِخْبَارِ وَلَا تَتَعَلَّقُ بِالْمَاضِي وَالْحَالِ وَالاسْتِقْبَالِ إِلَّا بِحَسَبِ التَّعَلُّقَاتِ وَالْإِضَافَاتِ كَالْعِلْمِ وَالْقُدْرَةِ وَسَائِرِ الصِّفَاتِ ، وَهَذَا الْكَلَامُ اللَّفْظِيُّ الْحَادِثِ الْمُؤَلَّفَ مِنْ الْأَصْوَاتِ وَالْحُرُوفِ الْقَائِمَةِ بِمَحَالْهَا يُسَمَّى كَلَامَ اللَّهِ تَعَالَى وَالْقُرْآنَ عَلَى مَعْنَى أَنَّهُ عِبَارَةٌ عَنْ ذَلِكَ الْمَعْنَى الْقَدِيمِ إِلَّا أَنَّ الْأحْكامَ لَمَّا كَانَتْ فِي نَظَرِ الْأُصُولِيٌّ مَنُوطَةٌ بِالْكَلَامِ اللَّفْظِى دُونَ الْأَزَلِى جُعِلَ الْقُرْآنُ اسْمًا لَهُ وَاعْتُبِرَ فِي تَفْسِيرِهِ مَا يُمَيِّؤُهُ عَنْ الْمَعْنَى الْقَدِيمِ لَا يُقَالُ التَّمُسِيرُ يَحُصُلُ بِمُجَرَّدِ ذِكْرِ النَّقْلِ فَلَا حَاجَةَ إِلَى بَاقِي الْقُيُودِ ؛ لأنَّا نَقُولُ التَّعْرِيفُ ، وَإِنْ كَانَ لِلتَّمْيِيزِ لَا بُدَّ وَأَنْ يُسَاوِيَ الْمُعَرَّفَ فَذَكَرَ بَاقِيَ الْقُيُودِ لِتَحْصِيلِ الْمُسَاوَاةِ . ترجمہ: یعنی ممتاز کرنا اس کو اس کے خواص کے ساتھ کیونکہ کلمہ اتنی طلب کیا جاتا ہے اس کے ذریعہ مٹی کی تمیز کو اس کے ساتھ
His statement: 'but its specification (tashkhis)' i.e., distinguishing it by its characteristics, because the word 'ay' is only used to demand the distinguishing of a thing by what is specific to it, whether it is a person or other. His statement: 'is applied to the eternal speech' as in his saying, peace be upon him: 'The Quran is the speech of Allah, Most High, uncreated.' It is an eternal attribute, incompatible with silence and defect, not of the genus of letters and sounds, does not vary into command, prohibition, and information, and does not relate to past, present, or future except in terms of relations and additions, like knowledge, power, and other attributes. And this created verbal speech, composed of sounds and letters subsisting in their loci, is called the speech of Allah and the Quran in the sense that it is an expression of that eternal meaning. However, since rulings, in the view of the usuli, are attached to the verbal speech and not the eternal, the Quran was made a name for it, and in its interpretation consideration was given to what distinguishes it from the eternal meaning. It cannot be said: distinction is achieved merely by mentioning transmission, so there is no need for the remaining restrictions, because we say: the definition, even if it is for distinction, must be equal (co-extensive) with the defined, so the remaining restrictions were mentioned to achieve equality. Translation: i.e., distinguishing it by its characteristics, because the word 'ay' is sought by it for the distinguishing of a thing with what is specific to it.
Source pages p. 447

Commentary
This passage is an expansion (شرح) on the definition of the Quran, distinguishing between the eternal divine speech (الكلام الأزلي) and the created verbal speech (الكلام اللفظي الحادث). The commentator explains that the term 'Quran' is applied to the eternal attribute of Allah, as in the hadith 'The Quran is the speech of Allah, uncreated' — it is an eternal attribute, not composed of letters or sounds, and does not vary. However, in usul al-fiqh, the term 'Quran' is used to refer to the created verbal expression (the recited, audible Quran) because legal rulings (الأحكام) are based on this verbal speech, not on the eternal attribute. This is a key distinction: the verbal Quran is an expression (عبارة) of the eternal meaning. Therefore, the definition of Quran in usul must include characteristics that distinguish this verbal expression from the eternal speech. The commentator then addresses a potential objection: why include additional restrictions in the definition if mere transmission suffices? He answers that definition must be co-extensive (مساواة) with the defined, so the extra restrictions ensure equality in scope. This reflects the Hanafi usul tradition's careful attention to definitional precision, ensuring that the term 'Quran' in legal discourse is limited to the transmitted verbal text, distinct from the eternal attribute. The Urdu translation at the end clarifies the meaning of 'tashkhis' (specification) and the function of the word 'ay'.

Key Terms
الكلام الأزلي (al-kalam al-azali): The eternal speech of Allah, an attribute that is uncreated, not composed of letters or sounds, and not subject to division into command, prohibition, etc., except by relations.
الكلام اللفظي الحادث (al-kalam al-lafzi al-hadith): The created verbal speech, composed of sounds and letters, which is an expression (عبارة) of the eternal meaning and is the subject of legal rulings.
تشخيص (tashkhis): Specification or distinguishing a thing by its particular characteristics.
مساواة (musawah): Co-extensiveness or equality between the definition and the defined; the definition must cover exactly the defined term without excess or deficiency.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the usuli (اصولي) consider the term 'Quran' as referring to the created verbal speech rather than the eternal attribute?
A) Because the eternal attribute cannot be known.
B) Because legal rulings (الأحكام) are attached to the verbal speech, not the eternal attribute.
C) Because the created speech is more easily transmitted.
D) Because the Prophet used the term for the verbal speech.

Key Takeaway
The Quran in usul al-fiqh is defined as the created verbal expression because legal rulings attach to it, and the definition must be co-extensive with the defined term.

Al-Quran as Source
intermediate
8 min
Why the Qur'an Cannot Be Defined (Hadd) Because It Is Shakhsi
Explaining the impossibility of defining the Qur'an as a shakhsi entity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Qur'an cannot be captured by a logical definition (hadd) according to Hanafi usul, and apply the concept of shakhsi to understand how the Qur'an is known only by direct indication.

Core Idea
The Qur'an is a specific composition (shakhsi) known only by recitation, not by universal definition.

In this passage, the commentator addresses a fundamental question in usul al-fiqh: why do usul scholars focus on verbal speech (kalam lafdhi) rather than eternal speech? The answer reveals a deeper point about the nature of the Qur'an itself—it is a unique, individual entity. This leads to a crucial insight: the Qur'an cannot be defined by a standard logical definition (hadd) because definitions apply only to universals, not to individuals. As you read, you will see how the commentator resolves an objection about the length of the definition and then demonstrates why the Qur'an is known only by direct indication or recitation from beginning to end.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish kalam nafsi and kalam lafdhi
Identify the two categories of divine speech: eternal inner speech (kalam nafsi) and contingent verbal speech (kalam lafdhi). Note which one usul scholars discuss and why.
Kalam nafsi is free from letters and sounds; kalam lafdhi is composed of letters and sounds.
2
Follow the objection and its answer
The objection claims that the definition of kalam lafdhi contains redundant attributes. Understand why the commentator insists that multiple attributes are needed for equality with the defined.
Distinction requires one attribute; equality requires all essential attributes.
3
Grasp the concept of shakhsi (individual)
A definition (hadd) is a universal statement covering parts; it cannot define an individual. The Qur'an, as a specific composition revealed to the Prophet, is shakhsi.
Shakhsi entities are known only by pointing or recitation.
4
Apply two interpretations
The passage offers two ways to understand 'the individual is not defined': either the Qur'an is analogous to an individual, or the term 'shakhsi' is used terminologically to mean the specific composition with its particularities.
Both lead to the same conclusion: the Qur'an cannot be defined.
5
Realize the practical implication
Knowledge of the Qur'an is obtained only by stating 'These words' and reciting from beginning to end. No concise definition suffices.
This has implications for how the Qur'an is used as a source of law.

الترويج لحَلَّ التَّوْضِيح وَالتَّلويج

کر دیا ہے کہ کلام از لی وہ ہے جو حروف سے آواز سے خاموش ہونے سے گونگا ہونے سے پاک ہو حروف سے پاک ہو کیونکہ غیر مخلوق ہے ماضی حال مستقبل سے خالی ہو کیونکہ اللہ کے لئے زمانہ نہیں ہے امرنبی و استفہام وغیرہ سے خالی ہو ہاں جب کلام کا تعلق مخلوق سے ہو تو حروف ، امر، نہی وغیرہ کے ساتھ تعبیر ہو اس کی کیفیت کیا ہے ؟ یہ اللہ جانتا ہے کلام لفظی وہ ہے جو مخلوق پڑھتی ہوا مر نہی وغیرہ سے تعبیر ہو اور اصوات و حروف سے مرکب ہے اور جس طرح ہم حادث ہیں یہ کلام بھی حادث ہے۔ اصولیین کلام لفظی سے بحث کیوں کرتے ہیں؟
الا ان سے سوال کا جواب دیا ہے۔
سوال:۔ یہ ہے کہ اصل قرآن تو ازلی ہے کلام لفظی تو کلام ازلی پر دال ہے تو اصولیوں کو ایسی تعریف کرنی چاہیئے جو کلام از لی اصلی پر صادق آئے حالانکہ اصولی کلام لفظی سے بحث کرتے ہیں؟
جواب:۔ اصولیین بحث کرتے ہیں اس اعتبار سے کہ احکام ثابت ہوں اور یہ احکام اس وقت ثابت ہونگے جب کلام میں امر نبی وغیرہ ہو اور یہ امر و نہی کلام لفظی میں ہوتے ہیں کیونکہ کلام ازلی میں امر و نہی نہیں پایا جاتا اس لئے اصولیین حضرات کلام لفظی سے بحث کرتے ہیں ۔
لا یقال : اعتراض یہ ہے کہ آپ فرماتے ہیں کہ مانقل الینا بیج سے یہ تعریف کی گئی ہے کلام لفظی کو ازلی سے ممتاز کرنے کے لئے تو پھر امتیاز کے لئے ایک وصف بھی کافی ہوتا ہے تو مانقل الینا کافی تھا پھر باقی قیود کی اور اتنی صفات کو ذکر کرنے کی کیا ضرورت تھی؟ جواب: تعریف میں دو چیزیں ہوتی ہیں (۱) امتیاز عن الغیر (۲) تعریف کی معرف سے مساوات اب امتیاز عن الغیر تو ایک وصف سے حاصل ہو جاتا ہے لیکن معرف کے ساتھ مساوات کے لئے ایک وصف کافی نہیں ہے بلکہ دیگر اوصاف کی ضرورت تھی اس لئے تعریف میں مانقل الینا بین دفتی المصاحف تو اتر ،، وغیرہ کی قیود و صفات کا اضافہ کیا تا کہ تعریف معرف کے مساوی ہو جائے۔
ثُمَّ أَرَادَ أَنْ يُبَيِّنَ أَنَّ الْقُرْآنَ لَيْسَ قَابِلا لِلْحَدِّ بِقَوْلِهِ ) عَلَى أَنَّ الشَّخْصِيَّ لَا يُحَدُّ فَإِنَّ الحَ هُوَ الْقَوْلُ الْمُعَرِّف لِلشَّيء الْمُشْتَمِلُ عَلَى أَجْزَائِهِ ، وَهَذَا لَا يُفِيدُ مَعْرِفَةَ الشَّخْصِيَّاتِ بَلُ لَا بُدَّ مِنْ الْإِشَارَةِ أَوْ نَحُوهَا إِلَى مُشَخْصَاتِهَا لِتَحْصُلَ الْمَعْرِفَةُ إِذَا عَرَفْتَ هَذَا فَاعْلَمُ أَنَّ الْقُرْآنَ لَمَّا نَزَلَ بِهِ جَبْرَائِيلُ صَلَوَاتُ اللَّهِ عَلَيْهِ فَقَدْ وُجِدَ مُشَخْصًا ، فَإِنْ كَانَ الْقُرْآنُ عِبَارَةٌ عَنْ ذَلِكَ الْمُشَخْصِ لَا يَقْبَلُ الْحَدَّ لِكَوْنِهِ شَخْصِيَّا وَإِنْ لَمْ يَكُنُ عِبَارَةً عَنْ ذَلِكَ الْمُشَخْصِ ، بَلْ الْقُرْآنُ هَذِهِ الكَلِمَاتُ الْمُرَكَّبَةُ تَرْكِيبًا خَاصًا سَوَاءٌ يَقْرَأُهُ جَبْرَائِيلُ أَوْ زَيْدٌ أَوْ عَمْرُو عَلَى أَنَّ الْحَقَّ هَذَا فَقَوْلُنَا عَلَى أَنَّ الشَّخْصِيَّ لَا يُحَدُّ لَهُ تَأْوِيلَانِ أَحَدُهُمَا أَنَّا لَا نَعْنِي أَنَّ الْقُرْآنَ شَخْصِيٌّ ، بَلْ عَلَيْنَا أَنَّ الْقُرْآنَ لَمَّا كَانَ هُوَ الْكَلَامُ الْمُرَكَّبُ تَرْكِيبًا خَاصًا فَإِنَّهُ لَا يَقْبَلُ الْحَدَّ كَمَا أَنَّ الشَّخْصِيَّ لَا يَقْبَلُ الْحَدَّ فَكَوْنُ الشَّخْصِيِّ لَا يُحَدُّ جُعِلَ دَلِيْلا عَلَى أَنَّ الْقُرْآنَ لَا يُحَدُّ إِذْ مَعْرِفَةُ كُلِّ مِنْهُمَا مَوْقُوفَةٌ عَلَى الْإِشَارَةِ أَمَّا مَعْرِفَةُ الشَّخْصِي فَظَاهِرٌ .
وَأَمَّا مَعْرِفَةُ الْقُرْآنِ فَلا تَحْصُلُ إِلَّا بِأَنْ يُقَالَ هُوَ هَذِهِ الْكَلِمَاتُ وَيُقْرَأُ مِنْ أَوَّلِهِ إلَى آخِرِهِ وَثَانِيهِمَا أَنَّا نَقُولُ لَا مُشَاحَّةَ فِي الاصْطِلاحِ فَنَعْنِي بِالشَّخْصِي هَذِهِ الْكَلِمَاتِ مَعَ الْخُصُوصِيَّاتِ الَّتِي لَهَا مَدْخَلٌ فِي هَذَا التَّرْكِيبِ فَإِنَّ الْأَعْرَاضَ تَنتَهِي بِمُشَخْصَاتِهَا إِلَى حَدَّ لَا يَقْبَلُ التَّعَدُّدَ ، وَلَا اخْتِلَافَ بِاعْتِبَارِ ذَاتِهَا ، بَلْ بِاعْتِبَارِ مَحَلَّهَا فَقَط كَالْقَصِيدَةِ الْمُعَيَّنَةِ لَا يُمْكِنُ تَعَدُّدُهَا إِلَّا بِحَسَبِ مَحَلَّهَا بِأَنْ يَقْرَأُهَا زَيْدٌ أَوْ عَمْرُو فَعَنَيْنَا بِالشَّخْصِي هَذَا وَالشَّخْصِيُّ بِهَذَا الْمَعْنَى لَا يَقْبَلُ الْحَدَّ فَإِذَا سُئِلَ عَنْ الْقُرْآنِ فَإِنَّهُ لَا يُعْرَف أَصَلا إِلَّا بِأَنْ يُقَالَ هُوَ هَذَا التَّرْكِيبُ الْمَخْصُوصُ فَيُقْرَأُ مِنْ أَوَّلِهِ إِلَى آخِرِهِ فَإِنَّ مَعْرِفَتَهُ لَا تُمُكِنُ إِلَّا بِهَذَا الطَّرِيقِ ، وَقَدْ عَرَّفَ ابْنُ الْحَاجِبِ الْقُرْآنَ بِأَنَّهُ الْكَلَامُ الْمُنَزَّلُ لِلْإِعْجَارِ بِسُورَةٍ مِنْهُ ، فَإِنْ حَاوَلَ
He has stated that the internal speech (kalam nafsi) is that which is free from letters, sound, silence, and dumbness; free from letters because it is uncreated; devoid of past, present, and future because Allah has no time; free from command, prohibition, and interrogation. Yes, when speech relates to creation, it is expressed with letters, command, prohibition, etc. What is its nature? Allah knows it. Verbal speech (kalam lafdhi) is that which the creation reads and expresses with command, prohibition, etc., and it is composed of sounds and letters. Just as we are contingent, this speech is also contingent. Why do the usuliyyun discuss verbal speech? He has answered the question from [someone]. Question: The original Qur'an is eternal; verbal speech indicates the eternal speech. So the usuliyyun should give a definition that applies to the original eternal speech, yet they discuss verbal speech. Answer: The usuliyyun discuss it with regard to rulings being established, and these rulings are established only when the speech contains command, prohibition, etc. And these command and prohibition are found in verbal speech because the eternal speech does not contain command and prohibition. Therefore, the usuliyyun discuss verbal speech. It is not said: An objection: You say that 'what is transmitted to us' (manqul ilayna) in this definition distinguishes verbal speech from the eternal; then one attribute would be sufficient for distinction. So 'what is transmitted to us' was enough; why were the remaining restrictions and attributes mentioned? Answer: A definition contains two things: (1) distinction from others, and (2) equality with the defined. Now distinction from others is achieved by one attribute, but equality with the defined requires more than one attribute. Therefore, in the definition they added restrictions and attributes like 'what is transmitted to us between the covers of the mushafs' etc., so that the definition equals the defined. Then he wanted to explain that the Qur'an is not subject to definition (hadd) by his statement: 'Because the individual (shakhsi) is not defined.' For the definition (hadd) is a statement that defines a thing by including its parts, and this does not provide knowledge of individuals; rather, one must point or similar to their individuators to obtain knowledge. When you know this, then know that when the Qur'an was brought down by Jibril, peace be upon him, it existed as an individual. If the Qur'an is that individual, it does not accept definition because it is individual. And if it is not that individual, rather the Qur'an is these words composed in a specific composition, whether read by Jibril or Zayd or Amr — and the truth is this — then our statement 'the individual is not defined' has two interpretations. One: We do not mean that the Qur'an is an individual; rather, because the Qur'an is speech composed in a specific composition, it does not accept definition just as the individual does not accept definition. So the fact that the individual is not defined is made evidence that the Qur'an is not defined, since knowledge of each depends on indication. As for knowledge of the individual, it is apparent. As for knowledge of the Qur'an, it is only obtained by saying 'It is these words' and reading it from beginning to end. The second: We say there is no dispute in terminology. So we mean by 'individual' these words with the particularities that enter into this composition. For accidents in their individuators reach a limit that does not accept multiplicity, and there is no difference in their essence, only in their locus, like a specific poem that cannot be multiplied except with respect to its locus — e.g., Zayd or Amr recites it. So we mean by 'individual' this, and the individual in this sense does not accept definition. So if asked about the Qur'an, it is not known at all except by saying 'It is this specific composition' and reciting from beginning to end, for its knowledge is only possible by this method. And Ibn al-Hajib defined the Qur'an as 'speech sent down for inimitability by a sura thereof.' So if he attempts...
Source pages p. 449

Commentary
This passage from the commentary (sharh) addresses two interconnected issues: first, why usul scholars discuss 'verbal speech' (kalam lafdhi) instead of the eternal speech, and second, why the Qur'an cannot be captured by a standard logical definition (hadd). The commentator begins with an Urdu explanation that eternal speech (kalam nafsi) is free from letters, sound, and temporal restrictions, whereas verbal speech is composed of letters and sounds and is contingent. The usul scholars focus on verbal speech because rulings (ahkam) such as command and prohibition only arise in verbal speech, not in eternal speech. The passage then moves into Arabic to argue that the Qur'an is not subject to definition (hadd) because, as a specific composition revealed to the Prophet, it is a 'shakhsi' (individual) entity. A definition, being a universal statement encompassing parts, cannot define an individual; one must point or indicate it directly. Two interpretations of 'the individual is not defined' are given: (1) the Qur'an is analogous to an individual in that it cannot be defined; one must say 'these words' and recite it. (2) In terminological sense, 'individual' here refers to the specific composition with its particularities, which, like a fixed poem, cannot be multiplied except by its reciter. Hence, the Qur'an is known only by direct indication. The passage ends with a reference to Ibn al-Hajib's definition of the Qur'an, but the text is cut off.

Key Terms
كلام نفسي (kalam nafsi): Eternal, uncreated speech of Allah, devoid of letters, sounds, and temporal attributes.
كلام لفظي (kalam lafdhi): Created verbal speech composed of letters and sounds, used to express commands, prohibitions, etc.
شخصي (shakhsi): An individual entity that cannot be multiplied or defined by a universal definition; known only by direct indication.
حد (hadd): A logical definition that states the essence of a thing by its parts (genus and differentia); cannot apply to individuals.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the primary reason the Qur'an cannot be defined by a logical definition (hadd)?
A) Because it is eternal and uncreated.
B) Because it is a specific composition (shakhsi) that can only be known by direct indication.
C) Because it contains both clear and ambiguous verses.
D) Because it was revealed in seven dialects.

Key Takeaway
The Qur'an is a unique composition (shakhsi) that cannot be captured by a universal definition; it must be known by direct recitation.

Defining the Quran: Shakhsi vs. Universal Definition
Refuting objections and critiquing Ibn al-Hajib's definition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify why the Hanafi school considers the Quran a shakhsi and apply the argument against circular definitions in usul al-fiqh.

Core Idea
The Quran, like an individual, cannot be captured by a universal definition; it is known by pointing or its unique characteristics.

In this passage, the author defends the Hanafi position that the Quran is a shakhsi—an individual particular that cannot be defined by a universal definition. Two objections are rebutted, and Ibn al-Hajib's alternative definition is shown to be circular. This discussion highlights a key principle in Hanafi usul: certain divine realities are known by indication, not by logical definitions that would apply to other things.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two objections
Read the passage to find the two objections against defining the Quran as a shakhsi. One objection concerns multiplicity of copies, the other concerns the requirement of a specific location.
Objections are introduced by the phrase 'وہ علیحدہ قرآن ہے'.
2
Analyze the first response
The author responds that the Quran is like a shakhsi, not a real shakhsi. Distinguish between real shakhsi and 'like a shakhsi'. Understand the role of indication (اشارہ) in distinguishing the Quran.
The author uses the example of pointing to the Quran from start to end.
3
Analyze the second response
The author redefines shakhsi in Hanafi terminology: specific words in a specific order, irrespective of location. Compare to the poem analogy to see how essence is preserved across multiple copies.
The poem of Imru' al-Qais is used as a qiyas.
4
Examine the critique of Ibn al-Hajib
Identify Ibn al-Hajib's definition and the circularity (dawr) that the author exposes. Understand why defining 'surah' as part of the Quran creates a loop.
Circularity occurs when the definiens uses the definiendum.
5
Conclude the Hanafi position
Synthesize that the Hanafi approach avoids circularity by not attempting a real definition, instead treating the Quran as a shakhsi known by pointing or distinguishing features.
This upholds the uniqueness of the Quran.

That is a separate Quran, and the one recited by Umar is a separate Quran. This is not a shakhsi (individual) because shakhsi is the mentioning of specific words in a specific order at a specific location, and here the location differs, causing multiplicity due to the difference of copies. Response (1): Our intention is that the Quran is not a shakhsi in reality but is like a shakhsi. Just as a shakhsi cannot be defined, its distinguishing characteristics are mentioned to distinguish it from others, similarly the Quran has no definition; it is distinguished by its qualities and characteristics or by a sign such as reading from beginning to end and pointing that this is the Quran. So it is not a shakhsi in reality but is like a shakhsi. Response (2): In our terminology, the mention of specific words in a specific order is called a shakhsi even if the locations are multiple. In our terminology, the words of the Quran, although they are accidental, due to the characteristics that when the words are specific and the order is specific, the shakhsi reaches a level where there is no multiplicity in its essence, only multiplicity in terms of location. For example, like the poem of Imru' al-Qais, with specific words in a specific order, i.e., the order of words and letters is the same; in this respect there is no multiplicity. Similarly, the Quran is the name of specific words and specific order, so there will be no multiplicity in its essence; however, there will be multiplicity in terms of location. Just as this poem is a shakhsi, the Quran is also a shakhsi in our terminology, and there is flexibility in establishing terminologies, and a shakhsi has no definition, so the definition of the Quran cannot be expressed. Allamah Ibn al-Hajib's definition is also not free from circularity. And 'Allamah Ibn al-Hajib defined [the Quran] as: 'The Quran is the revealed speech for inimitability, with a surah from it.' That is, the Quran is speech sent down by Allah which is miraculous, unparalleled, and consists of at least one surah. Allamah Ibn al-Hajib said that his definition does not involve circularity. The author said: if by this definition you intend to distinguish the Quran from other things, then distinguishing from others will occur. But if this is a real definition, circularity will be necessary because in a real definition, defining all parts is necessary. When asked for the definition of surah, you would say 'a part of the Quran,' so the definition of the Quran depends on surah, and the definition of surah depends on the Quran; this is circular and invalid. Hence, Ibn al-Hajib's definition is also not free from circularity. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 453

Commentary
This passage is a defense of the definition of the Quran as a shakhsi (individual particular) and a critique of Ibn al-Hajib's alternative definition. The author responds to two objections. First, an opponent argues that if the Quran is a shakhsi, then different copies (e.g., one recited by Umar) would be different Qurans, undermining its uniqueness. The author replies that the Quran is like a shakhsi but not a real shakhsi; it is distinguished by pointing or by its unique characteristics, much like an individual person is known by identification rather than a definition. Second, the opponent contends that the term shakhsi usually implies a specific location, whereas the Quran is present in many places. The author counters that in the Hanafi usul terminology, shakhsi is defined by specific words and order, not location. Just as a poem is considered a single shakhsi regardless of multiple recitations, the Quran's essence is unaffected by the multiplicity of copies. The author then turns to Ibn al-Hajib's definition of the Quran as 'revealed speech for inimitability comprising at least one surah.' He argues that this definition is circular because 'surah' is defined as a part of the Quran, and the Quran is defined by surah. Thus, Ibn al-Hajib's definition is no better. This discussion highlights the Hanafi position that the Quran, as the word of Allah, is unique and cannot be enclosed in a logical definition that would apply to other things. It underscores the principle that certain divine realities are known by indication rather than by universal definitions.

Key Terms
شخصی (shakhsi): An individual particular that cannot be defined by a universal definition, known only by pointing or distinguishing characteristics.
حد (hadd): A definition that comprises the essential constituents of a thing (muqawwimat) distinguishing it from others.
دور (dawr): Circularity in definition where the definiendum and definiens rely on each other, rendering the definition invalid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Because it includes 'surah' which is itself defined by the Quran.
B) Because it uses the term 'speech' which is undefined.
C) Because it does not specify the number of surahs.
D) Because it is not based on revelation.

Key Takeaway
The Quran, as divine speech, is a shakhsi that cannot be captured by universal definitions; attempts to define it logically risk circularity.

The Quran as a Specific Composition
Definition of the Quran as a specific composition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the Quran as the specific composition (al-muʾallaf al-makhṣūṣ) and explain why it does not vary with reciters, using the Hanafi usul principle that accidents are particularized by their loci.

Core Idea
The Quran is a specific composition; it remains the same whether recited by Zayd or Amr.

This passage defines the Quran in terms of its composition. The author argues that the Quran is a specific composition that does not change with different reciters, because accidents (like spoken sounds) are tied to their loci. This leads to a broader principle about the unity of entities independent of locus.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition
The Quran is defined as 'the specific composition' (المؤلف المخصوص). This means its identity is tied to the composition itself, not to any particular recitation.
The term 'specific' (مخصوص) emphasizes uniqueness and invariance.
2
Understand the argument from certainty
The author claims it is definitive (القطع) that what each of us recites is the same Quran revealed to the Prophet through Jibreel. This certainty grounds the identity claim.
If it were similar but not identical, the Quran would be multiple.
3
Apply the principle of accidents
Accidents (الأعراض) are particularized by their loci (محالها). Recitations are accidents that occur in different loci (tongues of reciters), so they multiply. But the Quran itself is not an accident; it is the composition.
This philosophical principle is used to separate the Quran's essence from its expressions.
4
Generalize to other compositions
The same applies to any book or poem: it is the name of that specific composition, regardless of who recites it. Similarly, sciences like grammar are defined by their specific rules.
This shows the principle is universal in Hanafi thought.
5
Conclude: unity in non-locus
The ultimate consideration is unity in that which is not a locus (الوحدة في غير المحال). The Quran's oneness is independent of its instances in recitation.
This supports the doctrine of the Quran's uncreated nature.

Source Text
